reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, input_size: int, hidden_size: int, output_size: int):
        """
        Initialize the Vanilla RNN model.
        
        :param input_size: The number of input features (int).
        :param hidden_size: The size of the hidden state (int).
        :param output_size: The number of output features (int).
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.input_size = input_size
        self.hidden_size = hidden_size
        self.output_size = output_size
        self.hidden = torch.randn((batch_size, hidden_size))
        
        # Define the RNN cell components (input to hidden, hidden to hidden, and hidden to output)
        self.i2h = nn.Linear(input_size + hidden_size, hidden_size)  # Input to hidden
        self.h2o = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size)  # Hidden to output
        self.tanh = nn.Tanh()  # Activation function for hidden state
    
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor, initial_hidden=None) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Forward pass of the Vanilla RNN.
        
        :param x: Input tensor of shape (batch_size, input_size).
        :param hidden: Hidden state tensor of shape (batch_size, hidden_size).
        :return: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, output_size), and the new hidden state.
        """
        if initial_hidden is not None:
            self.hidden.copy_(initial_hidden)
        self.hidden = self.hidden.to(x.device)
        combined = torch.cat((x, self.hidden), dim=1)  # Concatenate input and hidden state
        self.hidden = self.tanh(self.i2h(combined))  # Update hidden state
        output = self.h2o(self.hidden)  # Compute output
        return output

batch_size = 256
input_size = 16384
hidden_size = 16384
output_size = 8192
sequence_length = 256

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, input_size),torch.rand(batch_size, hidden_size)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [input_size, hidden_size, output_size]

We can avoid explicit concat by doing the matmul in pieces. For the i2h linear, the weight matrix W is shape (hidden_size, input_size + hidden_size). Instead of concatenating x and h, we can compute: ``` i2h_out = x @ W[:, :input_size].T + h @ W[:, input_size:].T + b ``` Then apply tanh, then h2o. Let me think about the most likely winning strategy: Keep the original i2h weight but avoid cat by using a persistent pre-allocated buffer. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, ...): ... self.i2h = nn.Linear(input_size + hidden_size, hidden_size) self.h2o = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size) # Pre-allocate concat buffer self.register_buffer('concat_buf', torch.empty(batch_size, input_size + hidden_size)) def forward(self, x, initial_hidden=None): if initial_hidden is not None: self.hidden.copy_(initial_hidden) self.hidden = self.hidden.to(x.device) # In-place concat into pre-allocated buffer self.concat_buf[:, :self.input_size] = x self.concat_buf[:, self.input_size:] = self.hidden h_new = torch.tanh(self.i2h(self.concat_buf)) self.hidden = h_new return self.h2o(h_new) ``` This saves the allocation in `torch.cat` but not the copies. Wait, that's way too high. Let me recalculate. 2 * M * K * N = 2 * 256 * 32768 * 16384 = 274,877,906,944 operations. At 19.5 TFLOPS = 19.5 * 10^12 ops/sec. So to get significant speedup, I need to do something more substantial. **Key insight**: The two smaller GEMMs for i2h can be run in PARALLEL on different CUDA streams! ```python stream1 = torch.cuda.Stream(device=x.device) stream2 = torch.cuda.Stream(device=x.device) with torch.cuda.stream(stream1): temp1 = torch.addmm(self.b_i2h, x, self.w_x.t()) with torch.cuda.stream(stream2): temp2 = torch.mm(self.hidden, self.w_h.t()) torch.cuda.synchronize() h_new = torch.tanh(temp1 + temp2) ``` If the GPU is not fully saturated by one GEMM (M=256 is relatively small), running two GEMMs concurrently could use more SMs and reduce the total time! For M=256, the occupancy might be limited. Each output block might handle e.g. 128x64 elements. With N=16384, that's 128 blocks in N. With M=256 and tile_M=64, that's 4 blocks in M. Total 512 blocks. For K=16384, each block does significant work. But 512 blocks might not fully saturate 108 SMs on A100, especially at FP32. Running two concurrent GEMMs means ~1024 blocks total, which might better saturate the GPU. In practice, concurrent GEMMs can give up to ~1.5x speedup for M=256 because single GEMM might not use all SMs efficiently. This is a very promising optimization! Can I do this with custom CUDA? Let me think about another angle: can I fuse the entire thing into a single custom kernel that does the matmuls using shared memory tiling? For the i2h step: M=256, K_x=16384, K_h=16384, N=16384. For each output element (i, j): result = bias[j] + sum_k x[i,k] * W_x[j,k] + sum_k h[i,k] * W_h[j,k] = bias[j] + dot(x[i,:], W_x[j,:]) + dot(h[i,:], W_h[j,:]) If a thread block handles a tile of output, say 64 rows (i) x 64 cols (j): - It needs to read x[64, 16384] and h[64, 16384] - that's 2 * 64 * 16384 * 4 = 8.4 MB. Too big for shared memory (164 KB). - So we tile along K. For a K-tile of 64: - Load x_tile[64, 64] and h_tile[64, 64] and W_tile[64, 64]? - Actually W is different for each output j, so we need W_x[j_start:j_end, k_start:k_end] and W_h similarly. - Each block would load W_tile[64, 64] for W_x and another for W_h. That's 2 * 64 * 64 * 4 = 32 KB. Fine. - Plus x_tile[64, 64] and h_tile[64, 64] = 32 KB. Total 64 KB. Good. - Iterate K_x/64 = 256 times and K_h/64 = 256 times. Total 512 iterations. - FLOPs per block: 2 * 64 * 64 * (16384 + 16384) = 268M - Memory per block: 512 iterations * (32KB + 32KB) = 32MB loaded - Arithmetic intensity: 268M FLOPs / 32MB = 8.4 FLOPs/byte. That's not great! It might be memory bound. - Wait, but W_x and W_h can stay in shared memory across M dimension if multiple blocks share the same j-range. Actually, standard GEMM tiling has much better AI. With a K-tile of 16 (using tensor cores), a block might compute 128x128 outputs with 128x16 A tiles and 16x128 B tiles. The memory traffic is much lower relative to compute. Doing this myself with proper tensor core usage via WMMA is extremely complex. The right answer is almost certainly to use cuBLAS. Wait, I just realized something critical about the reference implementation: ```python self.hidden = self.tanh(self.i2h(combined)) ``` Note: `self.hidden = self.tanh(self.i2h(combined))` updates self.hidden, but then: ```python output = self.h2o(self.hidden) ``` And returns `output`. But `self.hidden` is now the activated state. In the custom implementation, I'll follow the same. Here's my plan: 1. In `__init__`, store weights appropriately. 2. The key is that in C++, I can use streams or custom kernels more flexibly. Actually, what if I write a custom kernel that does the mat-vec products for ALL batch elements in a single kernel, without cuBLAS? For M=256, maybe I can have each thread handle one row of output (one batch element, one output feature). Wait, with N=16384 output features and K=16384 input features per output, a single thread doing the entire dot product would do 16384 FLOPs. With 256 * 16384 = 4.2M threads, each doing 16K multiplies. That's way too many threads and too little work per thread. With one block per batch element (256 blocks), and threads within the block handle different output features. If a block has 256 threads, each thread handles 16384/256 = 64 output features. Each output feature needs a dot product with 32768 elements (or two dot products with 16384 each). For one output feature: 2 * 16384 = 32768 FLOPs. For 64 outputs: 2M FLOPs per thread. At 1 TFLOPS per SM, that's 2 microseconds. But memory bandwidth for reading weights: 64 * 32768 * 4 bytes = 8.4 MB per thread? No, the weights are shared across threads in the block! Wait, if a block handles all 16384 outputs for one batch element: - All 16384 outputs share the same input x[16384] and h[16384]. - Each output j needs W_x[j, :] and W_h[j, :]. - A thread block with, say, 256 threads can compute 256 outputs in parallel. - Each thread does dot(x, W_x[j,:]) + dot(h, W_h[j,:]) for 64 different j values (since 16384/256 = 64). - Weights read per block: 16384 * 32768 * 4 bytes = 2 GB. Wait, that's huge! - Actually, each output j reads W[j, :] which is 32768 floats = 128 KB. - For 256 threads computing 64 outputs each, total weights read: 16384 * 32768 * 4 = 2 GB. - For 256 blocks (one per batch element): 256 * 2 GB = 512 GB total reads. - At 2 TB/s: 256 ms. That's impossibly slow. But wait, the weights are the same for all batch elements. So they're read 256 times. 512 GB of weight reads. That sounds terrible but maybe the cache helps? With A100's 40MB L2 cache, and reading 2GB of weights repeatedly, cache hit rate is 2%. So mostly going to HBM. This is why cuBLAS is so good - it tiles to maximize data reuse in shared memory and registers, minimizing global memory reads. Conclusion: I MUST use cuBLAS or similar optimized GEMM library for the matrix multiplications. A custom naive matmul will be much slower. **So the winning strategy is:** 1. Use PyTorch's cuBLAS wrappers (`torch.mm`, `torch.addmm`) for GEMMs 2. Write a custom CUDA kernel for element-wise fusion to reduce kernel launches and memory traffic for the intermediate steps 3. Use CUDA streams to run the two i2h GEMMs concurrently 4. In C++: ```cpp auto stream1 = c10::cuda::getStreamFromPool(false, x.device().index()); auto stream2 = c10::cuda::getStreamFromPool(false, x.device().index()); at::cuda::CUDAStreamGuard guard1(stream1); auto temp1 = at::addmm(b_i2h, x, w_x.t()); guard1.restore(); at::cuda::CUDAStreamGuard guard2(stream2); auto temp2 = at::mm(hidden, w_h.t()); guard2.restore(); // synchronize cudaStreamSynchronize(stream1); cudaStreamSynchronize(stream2); // Now on default stream auto h_new = fused_add_tanh(temp1, temp2, b_i2h); auto output = at::addmm(b_h2o, h_new, w_h2o.t()); return output; ``` Is `at::addmm` stream-safe? Let me analyze this problem carefully. We have a Vanilla RNN with: - batch_size = 256 - input_size = 16384 - hidden_size = 16384 - output_size = 8192 The forward pass does: 1. Concatenate input (batch_size, input_size) and hidden (batch_size, hidden_size) → (batch_size, input_size + hidden_size) 2. Apply linear i2h: (batch_size, input_size + hidden_size) @ (input_size + hidden_size, hidden_size) + bias → (batch_size, hidden_size) 3. Apply tanh activation → (batch_size, hidden_size) 4. Apply linear h2o: (batch_size, hidden_size) @ (hidden_size, output_size) + bias → (batch_size, output_size) So the combined input to i2h is size 16384 + 16384 = 32768. The i2h weight matrix is 32768 × 16384. This is a large GEMM problem. Let's think about the dimensions: - i2h: (256, 32768) × (32768, 16384) + bias - h2o: (256, 16384) × (16384, 8192) + bias For the i2h, we can avoid concatenation by splitting the operation: combined = [x, hidden] i2h(combined) = x @ W_x + hidden @ W_h + b Where W_x is (16384, 16384) and W_h is (16384, 16384) This avoids the concatenation step. Now, since the hidden state is stored as a parameter/attribute and gets modified, we need to be careful about the semantics. The RNN does for one step: hidden_new = tanh(x @ W_x + hidden @ W_h + b_h) output = hidden_new @ W_o + b_o Since we're doing a single forward step (not unrolling over sequence), this is essentially: 1. Two matrix multiplications for the i2h: x @ W_x and hidden @ W_h 2. Add them together with bias 3. Apply tanh 4. Another matrix multiplication for h2o Since the batch size is only 256 but the dimensions are very large (16384, 8192), this is compute-bound. We should use efficient GEMM operations. However, we're asked to write a custom CUDA kernel. PyTorch's default Linear layers should use cuBLAS which is highly optimized. So why would a custom kernel be faster? One thought: we can fuse the operations. Instead of launching multiple kernels for: 1. x @ W_x 2. hidden @ W_h 3. add + bias + tanh 4. hidden_new @ W_o We can potentially fuse some of these. But realistically, for large GEMMs on H100, cuBLAS is very optimized. Wait, let me re-read the problem. The reference implementation is using `nn.Linear` which will call cuBLAS. But there might be some inefficiencies: 1. The concatenation creates an intermediate tensor of size 256 × 32768 2. We need to process in tiles. Actually, a much better approach for an RNN step is: - For each tile of the output dimension (say 128 hidden features at a time): - Load x (256 × 16384) into shared memory in tiles - no, x is 64KB per batch element, times 256 = 16MB. This won't fit in shared memory per SM (228KB). So we need to tile across batch as well. The most natural tiling is: - Tile M dimension (batch) by M_tile (e.g., 16) - Tile N dimension (hidden) by N_tile (e.g., 128) - Tile K dimension (input) by K_tile (e.g., 64) For a block computing M_tile×N_tile outputs: - Load x tile: M_tile × K_tile = 16 × 64 floats = 4KB - Load W_x tile: K_tile × N_tile = 64 × 128 floats = 32KB - Compute partial sums - Repeat for all K tiles After finishing x contributions, do the same for hidden contributions using W_h. Then add bias, apply tanh. Then compute output = hidden_new @ W_o using similar tiling. But this is essentially reimplementing GEMM. Let me think about the numerical error: - Input x and hidden are in [0, 1) and ~N(0,1) - Weights from nn.Linear are initialized with small values - The dot product of K=16384 elements each ~0.01 gives sum ~1-10 - tanh of that is in [-1, 1] - Second matmul similar - If we use FP16 for the inputs to Tensor Cores with FP32 accumulation, the main error is the rounding of each input/weight to FP16. The rounding error is at most 0.5 ULP ~ 0.00003 for normalized values. With 16384 terms, worst-case error is ~0.5, but typical error is around sqrt(16384) × 0.00003 ≈ 0.004. Hmm, that might be too large for 1e-4 tolerance. Actually, let me reconsider. For random weights and inputs, the central limit theorem says errors accumulate with standard deviation proportional to sqrt(K). If each term has error ε, the sum has error ε × sqrt(K). For K=16384, sqrt(K)=128. If ε=2^-11 ≈ 0.0005 (FP16 rounding for typical values), then sum error ≈ 0.064. That's way above 1e-4. Hmm, but the tolerance is 1e-4 ABSOLUTE. So if the output is around 0.5, an error of 0.064 is 60× the tolerance. That's not going to work. Wait, let me recalculate more carefully. In FP16, the spacing between representable numbers around 1.0 is 2^-10 ≈ 0.000977. So rounding each element to FP16 introduces error up to ~0.0005. For a dot product sum_i a_i * b_i, if we round a_i to FP16 (and b_i is already FP16), each product a_i*b_i has error roughly |a_i| * ε_b + |b_i| * ε_a. If a_i, b_i ~ O(1/√K) due to initialization (Kaiming init has std dev = 1/√K for weights), then each product is O(1/K) and error is O(ε/K). The sum of K such terms has error O(ε/√K) by CLT. For K=16384, that's 0.0005 / 128 ≈ 4e-6, which is well within 1e-4. Let me check the initialization: PyTorch's Linear uses Kaiming uniform with a=sqrt(5). For input_size=16384, the weight std is roughly 1/sqrt(16384/3) ≈ 1/74 ≈ 0.0135. Actually, Kaiming uniform for fan_in uses bound = sqrt(1/fan_in) for some variants. The exact formula for nn.Linear default init is U(-sqrt(1/fan_in), sqrt(1/fan_in)) with a gain factor. Actually, PyTorch Linear init: `init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5))`. For kaiming_uniform with fan_in, std = sqrt(2 / ((1 + a^2) * fan_in)). For a=sqrt(5), that's sqrt(2/(6*fan_in)) = sqrt(1/(3*16384)) ≈ 1/222 ≈ 0.0045. So weights are small, around 0.0045 std. Inputs are uniform [0,1) with mean 0.5. The expected dot product for one output is K * mean(x) * mean(w) ≈ 16384 * 0.5 * 0 = 0 (mean of weights is 0). The variance is K * var(x) * var(w) ≈ 16384 * (1/12) * 0.0045^2 ≈ 16384 * 0.083 * 2e-5 ≈ 0.027. So the RMS value of the pre-tanh activation is around sqrt(0.027) ≈ 0.16. For the hidden update, we're adding two such dot products: x·W_x + h·W_h. If h is ~N(0,1), the W_h contribution has variance K * var(h) * var(w) = 16384 * 1 * 2e-5 ≈ 0.33, RMS ≈ 0.57. Actually, I need to be more careful. After the first step, the hidden state might grow in magnitude due to tanh not strictly bounding it to small values (tanh of 1 is 0.76, etc.). Over many steps it might saturate. But for our single-step evaluation, the activations before tanh are around 0.5-1.0 in RMS. After tanh, they're smaller. For the output step, hidden_new is bounded by tanh to [-1, 1], and W_o has similar variance, so the output is around sqrt(16384 * 1 * 2e-5) ≈ 0.57 RMS. Now, for FP16 error: the weights are ~0.0045. In FP16, the spacing at 0.0045 is 2^-17 ≈ 7.6e-6 (since 0.0045 is between 2^-8=0.0039 and 2^-7=0.0078, so exponent is -8, spacing is 2^(-8-10)=2^-18=3.8e-6). So weight rounding error is ~2e-6. For input x which is up to 1.0, spacing is 2^-10 ≈ 0.001. But for most inputs which are in [0,1), the average spacing is about 0.001/2 = 0.0005. Rounding error ~0.00025. Product of x and w: if we round both, the product is rounded to FP16. The product has magnitude up to ~0.0045. For 0.0045, spacing is 2^-18 ≈ 3.8e-6. The product error from rounding the inputs is roughly x*δw + w*δx ≈ 1*2e-6 + 0.0045*0.00025 ≈ 2e-6 + 1.1e-6 ≈ 3e-6. Then the product itself is rounded with error ~2e-6. Total per-product error ~5e-6. Sum of 16384 such terms: expected error sqrt(16384) * 5e-6 ≈ 128 * 5e-6 = 6.4e-4. Hmm, that's close to 1e-3 but still above 1e-4. Wait, this is the standard deviation. The actual error for a given output might be within 3 sigma ~ 0.002. That exceeds 1e-4. But addmm and GEMM typically use FP32 accumulation. If we store weights as FP16 but compute products in FP32 (which Tensor Cores do), the only error is from representing the weights and inputs in FP16. Actually, we can store weights as FP32 but convert to FP16 inside the kernel. The error comes from the conversion. Hmm, what about BF16? BF16 has much wider range but lower precision (7 bits mantissa vs 10 for FP16). The spacing at 1.0 is 2^-7 ≈ 0.008. That's actually worse for small weight values. For weights around 0.0045 in BF16: 0.0045 = 1.17 × 2^-8. In BF16, we have 7 mantissa bits, so 2^-8 with 7 bits of precision gives spacing 2^(-8-7) = 2^-15 ≈ 3e-5. That's much worse than FP16's 2^-18. So BF16 is worse for small values here. What about TF32? TF32 has 10-bit mantissa like FP16 but 8-bit exponent like FP32. It has the same precision as FP16 for normalized numbers (about 3 decimal digits). But TF32 is only available on newer hardware via specific APIs. Actually, if we use `wmma` or tensor core APIs with FP16, the error might be acceptable or not depending on the exact tolerance. Let me compute more carefully with actual simulation. Like: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, output_size): super().__init__() self.input_size = input_size self.hidden_size = hidden_size self.output_size = output_size # Pre-allocate hidden on GPU self.register_buffer('hidden', torch.zeros(batch_size, hidden_size)) self.W_x = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(hidden_size, input_size)) self.W_h = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(hidden_size, hidden_size)) self.b_h = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(hidden_size)) self.W_o = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(output_size, hidden_size)) self.b_o = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(output_size)) def forward(self, x, initial_hidden=None): if initial_hidden is not None: self.hidden.copy_(initial_hidden) # Use functional form for efficiency h_new = torch.tanh(F.linear(x, self.W_x, None) + F.linear(self.hidden, self.W_h, self.b_h)) out = F.linear(h_new, self.W_o, self.b_o) self.hidden.copy_(h_new) return out ``` But `F.linear` still calls into the same cuBLAS code. Let me draft a kernel idea. For the i2h step, instead of concat + GEMM, I'll pre-split the weight. Then for the forward: 1. Custom kernel 1: compute `new_hidden = tanh(x @ W_x + hidden @ W_h + b_h)` - Use a tile-based approach where each block computes a tile of the output - Use shared memory for the input tiles - For M=256, N=16384, K=16384 For a tile-based approach, we could do: ```cpp template<int BM, int BN, int BK> __global__ void rnn_i2h_kernel( const float* x, const float* hidden, const float* W_x, const float* W_h, const float* b_h, float* new_hidden, int M, int N, int K ) { // Each block computes a BM x BN tile of the output int block_m = blockIdx.x; int block_n = blockIdx.y; __shared__ float sA_x[BM][BK]; __shared__ float sA_h[BM][BK]; __shared__ float sB_x[BK][BN]; __shared__ float sB_h[BK][BN]; float accum[BN] = {0}; for (int k_tile = 0; k_tile < K / BK; k_tile++) { // Load x tile: M_tile rows, K_tile cols // Load W_x tile: K_tile rows, N_tile cols // Load hidden tile // Load W_h tile // Compute partial dot products for (int k = 0; k < BK; k++) { for (int n = 0; n < BN; n++) { accum[n] += sA_x[thread_m][k] * sB_x[k][n]; accum[n] += sA_h[thread_m][k] * sB_h[k][n]; } } } // Add bias, apply tanh, store } ``` But this naive approach has issues: 1. Bank conflicts in shared memory 2. No vectorized loads 3. No instruction-level parallelism 4. Extremely high register pressure for large BN 5. No Tensor Cores For decent performance, we'd need vectorized float4 loads, proper memory coalescing, double-buffering, unrolling, etc. Actually, for small M=256 and large N=16384, K=16384, a potentially simpler approach is to have one warp compute an entire row (one batch element's entire output). A warp has 32 threads. Each thread could compute N/32 elements by doing the dot product over K. For one row of output: - Thread i computes elements i, i+32, i+64, ... - Each thread maintains N/32 accumulators - We load K elements of x and hidden into shared memory or registers - We load columns of W_x and W_h in coalesced chunks Wait, for one row, we'd need to read 1 row of W_x (16384 floats) and 1 row of W_h (16384 floats), and multiple columns of W_x and W_h. Actually no: For output element j: new_hidden[j] = sum_k x[k] * W_x[k,j] + sum_k hidden[k] * W_h[k,j] + b[j] If one thread handles a contiguous chunk of j's, it needs to read W_x[:, j_start:j_end] and W_h[:, j_start:j_end]. For good memory coalescing, threads in a warp should read adjacent j values. So if warp computes elements j = warp_n * BN + lane_id, j += 32: - Each thread reads W_x[k, j], W_h[k, j] for its j, iterating over k. But this is very inefficient because we're iterating over the K dimension in the inner loop, and for each k we're doing strided memory access. The optimal GEMM access pattern is to load contiguous chunks of B (the weight matrix) and tiles of A (the input), then iterate over K in the inner loop. Actually, let me think about the exact memory access pattern for the weights. In PyTorch, `nn.Linear` stores weights as (out_features, in_features). So `self.i2h.weight` is (16384, 32768), stored row-major. For `input @ weight.T`, the effective GEMM is: A = input, shape (M, K) B = weight.T, shape (K, N) where weight is (N, K) in memory. So B is stored as (K, N) row-major, meaning B[k, n] is contiguous in n. This is perfect for tiling where we load tiles of B across the N dimension contiguously. For our split version: W_x is (input_size, hidden_size) = (16384, 16384). Stored as (16384, 16384) in memory. For `x @ W_x`, we need W_x as (16384, 16384) which means we want to load W_x[k, n] where n is the output dimension. In PyTorch, W_x[i, j] with i in [0, 16384) = input dim, j in [0, 16384) = hidden dim. `x @ W_x` means output[b, j] = sum_i x[b, i] * W_x[i, j]. Here W_x is accessed as W_x[i, j] where j is the fast-moving dimension. This is contiguous! Great. Similarly for W_h and W_o. So with pre-split weights, the GEMMs are all with contiguous weight access. Now, one insight: for M=256, the input activations x and hidden are small (64KB each). We can load all of x and hidden into shared memory per SM! For a block handling some output features, we can load x into shared memory once. Then we tile over the output dimension N. Actually, we don't even need to load all of x - just the batch elements we're processing. If a block handles BM batch elements and BN output features: - Load x[BM, :] and hidden[BM, :] into shared memory: 2 * BM * 16384 * 4 bytes - For BM=16, this is 2 * 16 * 64KB = 2MB - Shared memory per SM is 228KB 2MB is too much for shared memory. We need to tile K as well. For BM=16, BK=64: - Load x tile: 16 * 64 * 4 = 4KB - Load W_x tile: 64 * BN * 4 bytes. For BN=64, this is 16KB. - Total: 20KB per tile. That's fine. But we'd need double buffering and multiple tiles. For K=16384 and BK=64, we need 16384/64 = 256 iterations. In each iteration: - Load A tiles (x and hidden): 2 * 4KB = 8KB - Load B tiles (W_x and W_h): 2 * 16KB = 32KB - Compute: BM * BN * BK = 16 * 64 * 64 = 65K FMAs = 130K FLOPs For the compute: at 2 TFLOPS per SM (FP32), 130K FLOPs takes 65 microseconds... wait, no. 2 TFLOPS = 2e12 FLOPs/s. 130K FLOPs / 2e12 = 65 nanoseconds. But memory loads take time too. Actually, for BM=16, BN=64, BK=64: - Memory per block iteration: 40KB - At 2 TB/s shared memory bandwidth: 40KB / 2TB/s = 20 nanoseconds - But we need 256 iterations: 256 * 20ns = 5.1 microseconds per block - Total blocks for full output: (256/16) * (16384/64) = 16 * 256 = 4096 blocks - Total time (sequential): 4096 * 5.1us = 20.9ms At this tile size and assuming perfect shared memory bandwidth and overlap, we'd get: - Total FLOPs for i2h: 2 * 69B = 137.4B FMA = 275B FLOPs (each FMA is 2 FLOPs) Wait, for FMA: multiply-add is 2 FLOPs. So 16*64*64 = 65536 FMA per iteration. At 2 TFLOPS per SM, one iteration takes 65536 / 2e12 = 32.8 nanoseconds. - Per block: 256 * 32.8ns = 8.4 microseconds. - With 132 SMs, total throughput if perfectly parallel: 4096 blocks / 132 SMs * 8.4us = 261us. Actually, with enough parallelism (4096 blocks) and 132 SMs, each SM processes about 31 blocks. At 8.4us per block, that's 260us total. This would be 137.4B FLOPs / 260us = 528 TFLOPS. That's actually pretty good for FP32 on CUDA cores! But the reference with Tensor Cores might do this much faster. At 500 TFLOPS effective (TF32), the i2h alone would take 275ms... wait, 137.4B FLOPs / 500 TFLOPS = 0.275ms. That's 275 microseconds, not 260us - they're comparable. Wait, 137.4B FLOPs for i2h? Let me recalculate. The actual FLOPs: For each output: K multiply-adds. For two inputs: 2*K FMA per output. Total outputs: M * N = 256 * 16384 = 4,194,304. Total FMAs: 4,194,304 * 2 * 16384 = 137.4B FMAs = 275B FLOPs. So if our custom kernel does this in 260us, that's 275B / 260us = 1.06 PFLOPS. Wait, that's impossible for FP32 CUDA cores. Actually, the effective FP32 TFLOPS of H100 is 67 TFLOPS. At 67 TFLOPS for FP32: Time = 275B FLOPs / 67e12 = 4.1 ms. So our custom kernel on CUDA cores would take at least 4.1ms, and the reference with TF32 Tensor Cores would take 275B / 500e12 = 0.55 ms for i2h + some overhead. That's 8× difference. This confirms we need Tensor Cores. So how do we use Tensor Cores in a custom kernel? One option is to write a kernel that calls `wmma` for tf32. But tf32 `wmma` support might be limited. Another option: can we pre-convert weights and use FP16 Tensor Cores? Let's think about this more carefully with numerical analysis. Actually, PyTorch's default `nn.Linear` with FP32 inputs might use TF32 Tensor Cores automatically. If it does, then we get ~500+ effective TFLOPS. But TF32 has the same precision concerns as FP32 for values below 2^-14 because TF32 still has 8 exponent bits. Actually, no - TF32 has FP32 range but only 10 mantissa bits. Random uniform [0,1) values are all well-represented in TF32. The rounding error for each element is about 2^-23 * 2^10 = 2^-13 relative, or about 0.01% relative. For our application with tolerance 1e-4, this is usually fine. But if the reference uses TF32, then a custom kernel using FP16 Tensor Cores might introduce more error. However, if we use TF32 in our custom kernel too, we should match the reference's precision. Can we use TF32 in a custom kernel? Yes, through PTX mma instructions. But writing it is complex. The fastest approach might be: ```python def forward(self, x, initial_hidden=None): if initial_hidden is not None: self.hidden.copy_(initial_hidden) # Use a single fused kernel that computes tanh(x @ W_x + hidden @ W_h + b_h) # But wait, we can't do matmul inside a custom CUDA kernel without implementing GEMM. # Alternative: use torch.matmul and fuse the rest temp = torch.mm(x, self.W_x_t) + torch.mm(self.hidden, self.W_h_t) + self.b_h self.hidden = torch.tanh(temp) output = torch.mm(self.hidden, self.W_o_t) + self.b_o return output ``` With `W_x_t = W_x.t().contiguous()` precomputed in __init__, the GEMM becomes `output = input @ weight.T` where weight.T is contiguous. Actually, let me think about how to properly split: Original: combined = [x, h], output = combined @ W.T + b, where W is (16384, 32768). W = [W_x, W_h] where W_x is (16384, 16384) and W_h is (16384, 16384). combined @ W.T = [x, h] @ [W_x.T; W_h.T] = x @ W_x.T + h @ W_h.T. So `F.linear(x, W_x) + F.linear(h, W_h) + b` is correct. Kernels: 1. addmm(x, W_x.t(), bias=b_h): GEMM + write 2. addmm(hidden, W_h.t(), beta=1): GEMM + read/write accumulate 3. tanh: read/write 4. addmm(hidden_new, W_o.t(), bias=b_o): GEMM + write Total 4 kernels, no concat overhead. Let me think about what specific kernel design would be fast. For the exact dimensions M=256, K=16384, N=16384 or N=8192: - These are powers of 2, which is nice. - 16384 = 2^14, 8192 = 2^13. One approach that could work well is to use large tiles that take advantage of the dimensions. For example: - Each thread block handles 16 batch elements × 128 output features. - We divide K=16384 into K_tiles of 64. - Number of blocks: (256/16) × (16384/128) = 16 × 128 = 2048 blocks for i2h. - Each block does 16 × 128 × 64 FMA per K_tile. - With 256 K_tiles: total FMA per block = 16 × 128 × 16384 = 33.5M FMA. Wait, with so many blocks (2048) and 132 SMs, each SM gets about 15 blocks. At, say, 50% efficiency of peak FP32 (30 TFLOPS per SM), each block takes about 33.5M FMA / 30 TFLOPS = 1.1 ms? No, wait. 33.5M FMAs / 30e12 = 1.1 microseconds per block. 2048 blocks × 1.1us / 132 SMs = 17us. That's impossibly fast - faster than memory bandwidth allows! At 3.35 TB/s, reading the weights alone for i2h is 2 × 16384 × 16384 × 4 = 2.15 GB. Time = 2.15GB / 3.35TB/s = 0.64 ms. So the theoretical minimum is 0.64ms for memory-bound, or whatever the compute bound is. If we're compute-bound at 67 TFLOPS FP32: 275B FLOPs / 67TF = 4.1ms. If we use TF32 Tensor Cores at 500 TFLOPS: 275B / 500TF = 0.55ms. If memory-bound: 0.64ms. So the operation is actually near the memory-compute boundary for Tensor Cores. Since we probably can't write a Tensor Core kernel that outperforms cuBLAS, and the memory bandwidth is the ultimate limit anyway, the key advantage of a custom kernel might be fusion to save intermediate memory traffic. For the reference: 1. cat: writes 33.5MB 2. i2h GEMM + bias: reads 33.5MB (input) + 2.15GB (weights) + tiny (bias), writes 16.8MB 3. tanh: reads/writes 16.8MB 4. h2o GEMM + bias: reads 16.8MB (input) + 0.5GB (weights), writes 8.4MB Total intermediate writes/reads (excluding weights): 33.5 + 16.8 + 16.8 + 16.8 + 8.4 = 92.3 MB of activation traffic. For our fused kernel: - If we read x and hidden once (50MB total) and compute both contributions and the output in one go: - We read weights: 2.15GB + 0.5GB = 2.65GB - We write output: 8.4MB - No intermediate activation traffic! Total activation traffic: 50MB read + 8.4MB write = 58.4MB vs 92.3MB. Weight traffic: 2.65GB for both (same as reference: 2.15GB + 0.5GB = 2.65GB). So we save about 34MB of activation traffic. At 3.35TB/s, that's 10 microseconds. Not a huge win. But if we can do this using Tensor Cores... but cuBLAS already does. Hmm, maybe the real win is that the reference does one GEMM with M=256, K=32768, N=16384. A single GEMM of this size might achieve less effective TFLOPS than two smaller GEMMs due to algorithm selection or tiling inefficiency in cuBLAS for this specific shape. Actually, for M=256, K=32768, N=16384: - cuBLAS heuristics might not find an optimal algorithm for this M=256 "skinny" case. - The problem is that M=256 is small and K=32768 is large. The A matrix (256, 32768) is small (32MB) but B matrix is huge (2.15GB). In cuBLAS, for small-M large-K GEMMs, there are specific algorithms that try to maximize B reuse. But B is so large that it doesn't fit in L2 cache. So the kernel is essentially streaming B from memory and doing compute as fast as possible. For Tensor Cores, the kernel needs to keep the Tensor Core units fed. With M=256, each "wave" of blocks processes some portion of the output. For good performance, we want enough concurrent blocks to hide memory latency. With M=256 and typical tile sizes of 128×128 or 64×128: - Tiles in M: 2 or 4 - Tiles in N: 128 - Total tiles: 256 or 512 - That's plenty of parallelism for 132 SMs. Wait, but for cuBLAS, there's also kernel launch overhead, workspace setup, etc. For a single GEMM taking ~0.5ms, the overhead might be negligible. Actually, I just realized there might be a much simpler optimization. In the reference: ```python self.hidden = torch.randn((batch_size, hidden_size)) ``` This creates a tensor on CPU (by default since no device is specified). Every forward call does `self.hidden = self.hidden.to(x.device)` which, after the first call, should be a no-op but still involves a CUDA stream sync. If we define `self.hidden` as a `nn.Parameter` or `register_buffer` initialized on the correct device, or just register it properly, we avoid this overhead. More importantly, when we do `self.hidden.copy_(initial_hidden)` and then `self.hidden = self.tanh(self.i2h(combined))`, note that `self.tanh(...)` returns a NEW tensor. So `self.hidden` becomes a new tensor detached from the buffer. Next forward call, if `initial_hidden` is None, `self.hidden` is already on GPU, so `.to(x.device)` is a no-op. But the fact that `self.hidden` is not registered properly means it's not part of `model.parameters()` or `model.buffers()`, and won't be moved with `.to(device)` or `.cuda()`. For evaluation, the model is instantiated on CPU and inputs are on GPU. The first forward call will trigger `.to(x.device)` which copies `self.hidden` from CPU to GPU. But the weights are on CPU too! What if the reference implementation is slow because of the Python-level concatenation and object creation, and a pure PyTorch implementation that's better structured can be faster? For example: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, output_size): super().__init__() self.input_size = input_size self.hidden_size = hidden_size self.output_size = output_size # Properly register hidden as a buffer self.register_buffer('hidden', torch.zeros(batch_size, hidden_size)) # Split the linear layers self.i2h = nn.Linear(input_size + hidden_size, hidden_size) self.h2o = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size) self.tanh = nn.Tanh() def forward(self, x, initial_hidden=None): if initial_hidden is not None: self.hidden.copy_(initial_hidden) # Use addmm to avoid concatenation # h = W_h^T @ hidden + W_x^T @ x + b # For nn.Linear weight shape (out, in), W^T is (in, out) # x @ W_x^T where W_x = i2h.weight[:, :input_size] W = self.i2h.weight b = self.i2h.bias W_x = W[:, :self.input_size] W_h = W[:, self.input_size:] h = torch.addmm(b, x, W_x.t()) h = torch.addmm(h, self.hidden, W_h.t(), beta=1) self.hidden = self.tanh(h) output = self.h2o(self.hidden) return output ``` This avoids concat entirely and uses `addmm` with accumulation. What if we write a kernel specifically for M=256, using hardcoded dimensions for better register allocation and loop unrolling? Since the dimensions are fixed (input_size=16384, hidden_size=16384, output_size=8192, batch_size=256), we can hardcode these values into the kernel! With hardcoded dimensions, we can: 1. Unroll loops completely or partially 2. Use fixed-size arrays 3. Avoid dynamic indexing overhead 4. Optimize the exact register and shared memory usage For M=256, K=16384, N=16384 or N=8192: - We can process M in groups of, say, 8 (32 groups total) - We can process N in groups of, say, 256 (64 groups for N=16384, 32 groups for N=8192) - Total blocks: 32 × 64 = 2048 for i2h, 32 × 32 = 1024 for h2o. With 2048 blocks and 132 SMs, each SM gets ~15-16 blocks. Good parallelism. Shared memory size per block: for double buffering with BM=8, BN=256, BK=64: - A tile: 8 × 64 × 4 = 2KB - B tile: 64 × 256 × 4 = 64KB - Double buffer: (2KB + 64KB) × 2 = 132KB - Plus A2 tile for hidden: another 2KB × 2 = 4KB - Total: ~136KB per block. 136KB < 228KB maximum. Good. Each block computes 8 × 256 = 2048 output elements. Each output element requires K=16384 FMAs. Total FMA per block: 2048 × 16384 = 33.5M. If each block runs at 2 TFLOPS effective (FP32), time = 33.5M / 2e12 = 16.7 microseconds. For 2048 blocks on 132 SMs: 2048/132 × 16.7us = 259us. FLOPs = 137.4B FMA = 275B FLOPs. Effective performance = 275B / 259us = 1.06 PFLOPS. That's impossible for FP32! FP32 peak is only 67 TFLOPS. So at 67 TFLOPS: Time = 275B / 67e12 = 4.1ms. At 2048 blocks: each block takes 4.1ms × 132 / 2048 ≈ 264us. In 264us, each block does 33.5M FMA. FMA rate per block: 33.5M / 264us = 127 GFMA/s. Per thread (assuming 256 threads): 0.5 GFMA/s. That's low. Actually, the issue is that 264us per block is a lot, and with 256 threads, each thread does 33.5M/256 = 131K FMAs. At 1 FMA/cycle and 2GHz, that's 65us. But memory loads will dominate. For 264us per block, memory traffic per block: - W_x tiles: 64 × 256 × 4 × (16384/64) iterations = 256 × 16384 × 4 = 16.8MB At 264us, the bandwidth needed is 16.8MB / 264us = 63.6 GB/s per block. With 15 blocks per SM, total bandwidth per SM = 15 × 63.6 = 954 GB/s. For 132 SMs, total = 126 TB/s. That's way more than the 3.35 TB/s global memory bandwidth! So the operation is definitely memory-bandwidth bound for FP32, not compute-bound. The large amount of shared memory per block limits occupancy, reducing our ability to hide latency. At 3.35 TB/s, reading 2.15GB of weights for i2h takes 0.64ms. That's our lower bound for i2h. Even with Tensor Cores at 500 TFLOPS, the computation takes 0.27ms but reading weights takes 0.64ms. So the memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. Wait, but Tensor Cores might achieve much higher effective TFLOPS for large GEMMs because they have better data reuse. cuBLAS kernels are specifically designed to overlap computation with memory loads. Even though the weights don't fit in cache, they might be loaded in tiles that are reused across warps/blocks. Actually, for M=256, each row of W_x is used exactly once per forward pass (by one batch element). There is NO reuse of W_x across different batch elements unless multiple batch elements' computations are interleaved, which doesn't help because each batch element needs a different row of A (input) but the same W_x column... Wait, that's wrong. In GEMM C[m,n] = sum_k A[m,k] * B[k,n]: - Each row of A (one batch element's input) is used to compute one row of C. - Each column of B (one output feature's weights) is used to compute one column of C for ALL batch elements. So W_x[:, n] (column n) is used by all M=256 batch elements! This means if a block computes multiple batch elements for the same output feature(s), it can load a tile of W_x and use it for all batch elements in the tile. This is the key insight! For a block processing BM batch elements and BN output features: - Load W_x tile [k:k+BK, n:n+BN] once. - Use it for all BM batch elements. With BM=8 and BN=256, each W_x tile of 64×256 is loaded once and used for 8 batch elements. So the weight tile is reused BM times. The total weight bytes read per block = K × BN × 4 = 16384 × 256 × 4 = 16.8MB. Divided by reuse factor BM=8: effective weight traffic per output = 16.8MB / 8 = 2.1MB. Wait, no. The block still loads the entire K×BN tile. The reuse is in the compute, not in reducing memory reads. The memory read is still K×BN×4 bytes. But the computation uses it BM times. So the arithmetic intensity is: - Compute: BM × BN × K FMA = 8 × 256 × 16384 = 33.5M FMA - Memory: read A tile = BM × K × 4 = 8 × 16384 × 4 = 512KB, read B tile = K × BN × 4 = 16384 × 256 × 4 = 16.8MB, read bias = BN × 4 = 1KB, write C = BM × BN × 4 = 8KB - Total memory: ~17.3MB - FMA per byte: 33.5M / 17.3MB = 1.94 FMA per byte. - At 2 FLOPs per FMA: 3.88 FLOPs per byte. For H100, compute/bandwidth ratio at 67 TFLOPS FP32 / 3.35 TB/s = 20 FLOPs per byte. At 989 TFLOPS TF32 / 3.35 TB/s = 295 FLOPs per byte. Since our AI is only 3.88 FLOPs/byte, this operation is heavily memory-bound even for FP32, and extremely memory-bound for Tensor Cores. Wait, but this assumes we read B from global memory for every tile. In reality, we might not read all of K×BN if we can keep some of it in cache. But with K=16384 and BN=256, one tile is 16.8MB, which exceeds L2 cache (50MB) but not by too much. Actually 16.8MB fits easily in L2! And with multiple blocks on the same SM or different SMs, L2 can hold multiple tiles. Actually, if BN=256 and we have 64 blocks in the N dimension, total weight for all N blocks = K × N × 4 = 1.07GB. This doesn't all fit in L2. But each block only needs its own K×BN slice. If blocks are scheduled well, as one block finishes and another starts on the same SM, the new block might reuse some of the previous block's L2 cache if their N ranges overlap. But they don't overlap. However, if multiple blocks are running concurrently on the same SM (which requires many resident blocks), they might compete for cache. For memory-bound GEMM, the key metric is actual memory bandwidth utilization. A well-optimized kernel can use ~80-90% of peak bandwidth. cuBLAS is very good at this. So if the operation is memory-bound, and cuBLAS already achieves near-peak bandwidth, there's little room for a custom kernel to improve through fusion or better tiling. Let me think about this differently. Maybe I can write a kernel that does the two i2h GEMMs using vectorized dot products with good memory coalescing, and it's competitive because the batch size is small. For each output feature j (0 to 16383): For each batch element b (0 to 255): sum = b_h[j] For k from 0 to 16383: sum += x[b, k] * W_x[k, j] + hidden[b, k] * W_h[k, j] hidden_new[b, j] = tanh(sum) If we have each thread compute one (b, j) element: - Thread loads x[b, :] (16384 floats) and hidden[b, :] (16384 floats) - Thread loads W_x[:, j] (16384 floats) and W_h[:, j] (16384 floats) - Computes dot products But each thread would need its own 64KB of input + 64KB of weights = 128KB. Won't fit in registers. However, if we have a warp compute one batch element b for 32 output features: - Load x[b, :] into shared memory once per warp/block: 64KB - Each thread in warp computes one output feature j - Thread loads W_x[:, j] and W_h[:, j] from global memory - Since threads in warp load adjacent j values, W_x[k, j:j+31] is coalesced for fixed k. To compute the dot product, we iterate k from 0 to 16383: Load x[b, k] from shared memory (one float, broadcast to all threads) Load W_x[k, j] for j in lane_id, lane_id+32, ... from global memory Load W_h[k, j] similarly Do multiply-add For k loop, each thread loads W_x[k, its_j] in a coalesced manner if threads access adjacent j values. But in the inner loop over k, for each k we're accessing different memory locations in W_x. This is NOT coalesced within the loop - it's strided across k! Wait, for a fixed j, W_x[0, j], W_x[1, j], W_x[2, j], ... are NOT contiguous in memory. They are strided by the leading dimension (16384). The stride between W_x[k, j] and W_x[k+1, j] is 16384 floats = 64KB. So as we iterate over k, each thread is accessing memory with a stride of 64KB. This is terrible for cache because each access brings in a cache line (128 bytes = 32 floats), but we only use one float before jumping 64KB away. The next access is likely a cache miss. For good performance, we need to load tiles of W_x into shared memory, or at least access contiguous memory. If we tile over k in chunks of 32: - Load W_x[k:k+32, j:j+warp_size] for a tile of 32×32 floats - This tile is 4KB and can be loaded coalesced - Iterate over the 32 elements in the tile This is the standard tiled GEMM approach. But wmma requires data to be loaded into special fragment structures. The data layout is complex. Actually, there's another approach: use vectorized dot products with `dp4a` or similar instructions. For FP32, we can use `fma` with 4-wide vectors (float4) to improve instruction throughput. `float4` loads give 4 floats per instruction. Then we can unroll the K loop. Let me try to write a kernel: ```cuda template <int BM, int BN, int BK> __global__ void rnn_i2h_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h, const float* __restrict__ W_x, const float* __restrict__ W_h, const float* __restrict__ b, float* __restrict__ out, int M, int N, int K) { // Block computes output tile [bm:bm+BM, bn:bn+BN] int bm = blockIdx.x * BM; int bn = blockIdx.y * BN; int tid = threadIdx.x; int n_threads = blockDim.x; // Each thread accumulates a subset of the BN outputs for each batch element // Let's have threads distributed as (thread_m, thread_n) // For simplicity, each thread handles all BM batch elements for a slice of BN int threads_per_n = BN / 4; // each thread computes 4 consecutive n values int thread_n_start = (tid % threads_per_n) * 4; int thread_m = tid / threads_per_n; if (thread_m >= BM) return; // some threads might be idle float sum[4] = {0}; // Iterate over K in tiles for (int bk = 0; bk < K; bk += BK) { // Load x and h tiles for this batch element into shared memory // (协作加载,需要同步) __shared__ float sA_x[BM][BK]; __shared__ float sA_h[BM][BK]; __shared__ float sB_x[BK][BN]; __shared__ float sB_h[BK][BN]; // Collaborative load // ... __syncthreads(); for (int k = 0; k < BK; k++) { float ax = sA_x[thread_m][k]; float ah = sA_h[thread_m][k]; #pragma unroll for (int n = 0; n < 4; n++) { sum[n] += ax * sB_x[k][thread_n_start + n]; sum[n] += ah * sB_h[k][thread_n_start + n]; } } __syncthreads(); } // Add bias, apply tanh, store int base_idx = (bm + thread_m) * N + bn + thread_n_start; for (int n = 0; n < 4; n++) { float val = sum[n] + b[bn + thread_n_start + n]; out[base_idx + n] = tanhf(val); } } ``` This is a basic tiled kernel. For better performance, we'd vectorize the shared memory loads and use more threads. But I realize this is getting too involved for text-based iteration. For output element (b, j): - We need x[b, :] and h[b, :] and W_x[:, j], W_h[:, j] - If we tile by batch blocks of size BM, we load x[bm:bm+BM, :] into shared memory? That's too big. - Instead, tile by K: for each K chunk, load x[b, k:k+CK] and W_x[k:k+CK, j] into registers/shared mem, compute partial dot products. With a 1D grid over batch×output elements, and each thread computing one (b,j): - Number of threads = M × N = 4M (for i2h) - Each thread loops over K in chunks. - For each chunk, the thread loads x[b, k:k+CK] and W_x[k:k+CK, j]. But W_x[k:k+CK, j] is strided by N=16384. Loading a chunk requires CK strided loads. What if we transpose W_x so that W_x_t is (N, K)? Then W_x_t[j, k] is contiguous in k. If W_x_t is stored as (N, K), then `x[b, :] * W_x_t[j, :]` is a dot product of two contiguous vectors! For the dot product x[b, :] · W_x_t[j, :], if both are contiguous, we can use vectorized dot product with float4. YES! This is the key insight. If we transpose the weights during initialization so that W_x is (K, N) = (16384, 16384) but accessed as W_x[k, j], then for fixed j, W_x[:, j] is strided. But if we transpose to W_x_t of shape (N, K) = (16384, 16384), then W_x_t[j, k] is contiguous in k! Then for `x @ W_x` where x is (M, K) and W_x is (K, N) in standard layout, the GEMM is C = x @ W_x. If we store W_x as (N, K) instead, then C = x @ W_x^T. Wait, in PyTorch `nn.Linear`, the weight is (out_features, in_features). So W is (16384, 32768). For `input @ W.T`, we need W.T which is (32768, 16384). If we store W.T as our weight, then `input @ weight` uses weight as (32768, 16384). For the split version: W_x is a slice of W, shape (16384, 16384). W_x.T is (16384, 16384). For `x @ W_x.T`, if we store `W_x_t = W_x.T` during initialization (contiguous), then the GEMM becomes `x @ W_x_t` where both x and W_x_t have K=16384 as the inner dimension. This is just a standard GEMM. But for our custom kernel, we want to avoid writing a full GEMM. What if we process the computation at the output level? For each output element (b, j): sum_k x[b, k] * W_x_t[j, k] If W_x_t is (N, K), then W_x_t[j, :] is contiguous. x[b, :] is also contiguous. This is a dot product of two contiguous vectors! We can compute this with a simple reduction or even call `cublasSdot`. But launching one `cublasSdot` per output element would be terrible overhead. What if each thread computes one dot product using a simple loop with float4? - Thread (b, j) loads x[b, k:k+4] and W_x_t[j, k:k+4] as float4. - Accumulates the dot product. - After K=16384 iterations, stores the result. For 4,194,304 threads (256 × 16384), this is way more threads than the GPU has. The GPU has about 16,512 CUDA cores on H100. If each thread does 16384 FMAs, total work is 4.2M × 16K = 68B FMAs. With 4 threads per float4 dot product iteration, each thread does 4096 iterations. Each iteration: - Load float4 from x: 16 bytes - Load float4 from W_x_t: 16 bytes - 4 FMAs So each thread does: 4096 × 16 = 64KB reads from x, 64KB reads from W_x_t, and 16K FMAs. Total W_x_t read = 4.2M threads × 64KB = 268TB? No, because threads run sequentially in warps, not all at once. Actually, for threads in the same warp with different j values, they read different W_x_t rows. These are strided by K floats = 64KB. So consecutive threads read addresses 64KB apart - NOT coalesced! This is terrible. To fix this, we need adjacent threads to read the same W_x_t row (same j) but different k, or adjacent threads to read adjacent k within the same j. Wait, if we assign thread blocks by (b, j_group), and threads within a block handle different k values for the same (b, j), then we can coalesce loads of W_x_t. This is essentially a parallel reduction for each dot product. The overhead is high. Alternatively, a block computes outputs for one b and a group of j values. Hmm, the strided access is the fundamental problem. To get coalesced access to W_x_t, adjacent threads must have the same j and adjacent k. Wait, but in a proper GEMM, x should be reused across output features. If warps processing the same m_start but different n_start share the same A data, they should load it cooperatively. In our block design, if a block has warps processing the same M tile but different N tiles, they can all share the same A data loaded into shared memory. For example: - Block handles M_tile=16, with warps processing N tiles n=0,16,32,... (say 8 warps, 8 N tiles = 128 features). - The block loads A tile of 16×BK into shared memory once per K iteration, and all 8 warps use it. - Each warp loads its own B tile of BK×16 from shared memory or global memory. This is the standard approach. With BK=8 (matching wmma), we'd have 2048 K iterations. Loading A each time is inefficient. Better to use BK=64 or 128 in shared memory, then each warp does 8 or 16 mma operations per shared memory load. Standard wmma GEMM pipeline: 1. Load A_shared[BM][BK] and B_shared[BK][BN] from global memory. 2. Synchronize. 3. For each k_step in [0, BK/8): a. Load A_frag from A_shared[:, k_step*8:(k_step+1)*8] b. Load B_frag from B_shared[k_step*8:(k_step+1)*8, :] c. mma_sync 4. Move to next K tile. This reduces global memory loads. With BK=64: - A_shared: 16 × 64 = 4KB - B_shared: 64 × 128 (for BN=128) = 32KB - Total: 36KB. Actually, I just realized that transposing weights to (K, N) means `x @ W_x` becomes dot products of rows of x with rows of W_x_t. For fixed j, W_x_t[j, :] is contiguous. Let me write a kernel for step 1 with: - BlockDim = (16, 16) = 256 threads - BM = 16, BN = 64, BK = 64 - Each thread computes (BM/16) × (BN/16) = 1 × 4 output elements - Threads are arranged as (ty, tx) where ty handles batch elements, tx handles output features - Thread (ty, tx) computes outputs for batch elements bm+ty and features bn+tx*4 to bn+tx*4+3 Wait, BM=16 and block has 16 threads in y-dimension = 1 batch element per thread in y. So each thread handles 1 batch element. BN=64 and block has 16 threads in x-dimension, so each thread handles 64/16 = 4 output features. This means each thread computes 4 dot products (one output feature each) for its batch element. For each dot product, it needs sum_k x[b, k] * W_x_t[j, k]. If we load W_x_t for BN=64 features into shared memory (64 × BK floats), and x for BM=16 batch elements into shared memory (16 × BK floats), then each thread does: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { float sum = 0; for (int k = 0; k < BK; k++) { sum += sA[ty][k] * sB[k][tx * 4 + j]; } accum[j] += sum; } ``` This requires sB to be in shared memory with shape [BK][BN]. For BK=64, BN=64: 64×64 = 4096 floats = 16KB. Wait, but W_x_t[j, k] for our layout is row j, column k. So W_x_t[j_start + jj, k_start + kk] is accessed as we need. If we store W_x_t as (N, K) row-major, then W_x_t[jj, kk] is at position jj*K + kk, which is contiguous in kk. sB[kk][jj] means sB is stored with kk as the first dimension. So sB[kk][jj] is contiguous in jj. BUT W_x_t[jj][kk] is contiguous in kk. So to load from W_x_t into sB, we need to transpose! If we keep W_x in standard (N, K) layout and access W_x_t[j][k], threads in a warp accessing different j but same k will have strided access (stride K). NOT coalesced. If we instead store W_x in (K, N) layout (which is the transpose of what we want for dot products), then for W[k][j], accessing different j with same k is coalesced. But our dot product needs W[j][k] or W^T[k][j]. Hmm, this is getting complicated. Let me think about this more carefully. In standard GEMM with C = A @ B^T: - A is (M, K) - B is (N, K) - already transposed! - C[m, n] = sum_k A[m, k] * B[n, k] This is perfect for our use case! If we store W_x as (N, K) = (16384, 16384) with row n and column k, then C = x @ W_x^T where W_x is (K, N) originally. Wait. Original: C = x @ W_x where x is (M, K) and W_x is (K, N). In PyTorch matmul: the inner dimension is K. So x[b, k] * W_x[k, j]. W_x in memory: W_x[k, j] is contiguous in j (since PyTorch is row-major in the last dimension, but here W_x from Linear is (out, in) = (N, K)). So W_x[k, j] in standard C indexing is at position k * N + j. For `x @ W_x.T` where W_x is (N, K): W_x.T is (K, N) physically but since W_x is stored row-major, W_x.T has W_x.T[k, j] = W_x[j, k], and the memory layout of W_x.T is such that consecutive k are NOT contiguous for fixed j. Actually, W_x.T[k, j] accesses memory at k * 1 + j * K? No. PyTorch tensor W_x of shape (N, K). Element (j, k) is at memory offset j*K + k. W_x.T is a view with shape (K, N). Element (k, j) of W_x.T accesses memory offset j*K + k. For fixed k and varying j, offsets are j*K + k, so stride is K. NOT contiguous. For fixed j and varying k, offsets are j*K + k, so stride is 1. Contiguous. In `x @ W_x.T`: x is (M, K), W_x.T is (K, N). The matmul is C[m, j] = sum_k x[m, k] * W_x.T[k, j]. For the product, we access x[m, k] (contiguous in k) and W_x.T[k, j] for varying k. But W_x.T[k, j] for varying k and fixed j has memory offsets j*K + k. These are CONTIGUOUS in k! So for `x @ W_x.T`: - x[m, :] is contiguous - W_x.T[:, j] = W_x[j, :] is contiguous (since W_x is row-major!) If we store the weight as W_t of shape (N, K) in standard memory layout, then W_t[j, k] is contiguous in k. Then `x @ W_t` computes C[m, j] = sum_k x[m, k] * W_t[j, k]. This is NOT a standard matrix multiply in terms of layout because both x[m, :] and W_t[j, :] are row vectors. To make it a standard matrix multiply, we'd want: - A = x, shape (M, K) - B = W_t^T, shape (K, N) Then C = A @ B = x @ W_t^T. But W_t^T in memory has layout W_t^T[k, j] = W_t[j, k], which is NOT contiguous in k for fixed j. Actually, the standard matmul `A @ B` with A (M, K) and B (K, N) accesses: - A[m, k] contiguous in k - B[k, n] contiguous in n for fixed k But we need B[k, n] for varying k and fixed n. PyTorch's matmul handles this via cuBLAS which supports transposes. cuBLAS can compute `C = A * op(B)` where op(B) is the transpose. This means cuBLAS reads B as B^T, which in our case means accessing B[n, k] which is contiguous in k. So if W_t is shape (N, K), then cuBLAS can compute `x @ W_t` as a "NT" (A normal, B transposed) GEMM where A=x (M, K) and B=W_t (N, K) with B treated as transposed. This accesses: - A[m, k] contiguous in k - B[n, k] with n varying across threads and k as the dot product dimension In cuBLAS, this maps to `C = A * B^T` if B is (N, K). Wait, if B is (N, K) and we treat it as transposed for the GEMM, B^T is (K, N). So it's equivalent to `A @ B`. But the actual memory access is B[n, k] which is contiguous in k. This is great for our dot products. For our custom kernel, if we store weights as (N, K), then to compute dot product for output feature j, we read weights[j, :] which is contiguous! And a block computing outputs j_start:j_start+BN reads weight rows j_start:j_start+BN. These are strided by K in memory (since weight[j, k] is at offset j*K + k, consecutive j are K apart). To coalesce these loads, we should tile K first: - For a K chunk k:k+BK, load weight[j_start:j_start+BN, k:k+BK] into shared memory. - This is a BN × BK tile. - If BN=64, BK=64: 4096 floats = 16KB. - To load this coalesced: thread (j, k) loads weight[j_start + j, k_start + k]. - If we organize threads as (k, j) with k varying faster in threadIdx.x, then consecutive threads load consecutive k for the same j. But we want coalescing! For coalesced global load, consecutive threads should access consecutive memory. Memory layout: weight[j, k] is at offset j*K + k. If thread tx loads weight[j, k0 + tx], then consecutive tx load consecutive memory (for fixed j). But we have 2D data. If we flatten as (j, k) with k varying fastest, then thread tx corresponds to (tx / BK, tx % BK). Consecutive threads have consecutive k but same j. These are consecutive in memory! So if we map thread tx (0..255) to load sB[tx/BK][tx%BK] from weight[j_start + tx/BK][k_start + tx%BK], this is fully coalesced! Great! So for BN=64, BK=64: - tx 0..63: j = j_start + 0, k = k_start + 0..63. Consecutive threads load consecutive k. Coalesced! - tx 64..127: j = j_start + 1, k = k_start + 0..63. Coalesced within this group. - But between tx=63 and tx=64, memory jump is from weight[j_start, k_start+63] to weight[j_start+1, k_start]. That's a jump of K - 63 floats = 64KB - 252 bytes. NOT coalesced across groups! This means we have 64 separate coalesced segments per warp. On modern GPUs, a warp can handle multiple segments, but there might be some overhead. Actually, loads within a warp are issued per 32 threads. So threads 0-31 load weight[j_start, k_start:k_start+31] which is coalesced. Threads 32-63 load weight[j_start, k_start+32:k_start+63] which is also coalesced. So for k within one j, it's fine. But if we have more than 64 threads (we have 256), then some warps handle different j values. That's fine as long as each warp's loads are coalesced. For warp w, threads load weight[j_start + j_warp, k_start + k_lane]. If k_lane varies 0..31, these are fully coalesced for that warp. The next warp loads a different j, which is a different 64KB segment. But warps execute independently. This is fine. So our loading is coalesced within each warp. Great, now we can write a clean kernel! Let me design the kernel formally: ```cuda __global__ void rnn_step1_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, // (M, K) const float* __restrict__ h, // (M, K) const float* __restrict__ W_x, // (N, K) - transposed weight const float* __restrict__ W_h, // (N, K) - transposed weight const float* __restrict__ b, // (N,) float* __restrict__ out, // (M, N) int M, int N, int K ) { // Block computes tile [bm:bm+BM, bn:bn+BN] int bm = blockIdx.x * BM; int bn = blockIdx.y * BN; // Thread computes one or more outputs int tid = threadIdx.x; int n_threads = blockDim.x; // Distribute threads across output features and batch elements // Each thread handles: // batch_idx = bm + (tid % BM) // feat_idx_start = bn + (tid / BM) * FEATS_PER_THREAD int batch_offset = tid % BM; int feat_offset = (tid / BM) * FEATS_PER_THREAD; int b_idx = bm + batch_offset; int f_idx = bn + feat_offset; float sum[FEATS_PER_THREAD] = {0.0f}; // Shared memory for input and weight tiles // sA_x: [BM][BK] // sA_h: [BM][BK] // sB_x: [BN][BK] - wait, if we load W_x as BN rows × BK cols, and store by (BN, BK) // But for our dot product, we want to iterate k and access sA[batch][k] and sB[feat][k]. // If sB is [BN][BK], then sB[feat][k] is perfect. __shared__ float sA_x[BM][BK]; __shared__ float sA_h[BM][BK]; __shared__ float sB_x[BN][BK]; __shared__ float sB_h[BN][BK]; for (int bk = 0; bk < K; bk += BK) { // Load A tiles (x and h) for this batch group // Each thread loads several elements for (int i = tid; i < BM * BK; i += n_threads) { int r = i / BK; int c = i % BK; sA_x[r][c] = x[(bm + r) * K + bk + c]; sA_h[r][c] = h[(bm + r) * K + bk + c]; } // Load B tiles (W_x and W_h) for this feature group for (int i = tid; i < BN * BK; i += n_threads) { int r = i / BK; // feature within BN (0..BN-1) int c = i % BK; // k within BK (0..BK-1) sB_x[r][c] = W_x[(bn + r) * K + bk + c]; sB_h[r][c] = W_h[(bn + r) * K + bk + c]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute partial dot products if (b_idx < M) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < BK; k++) { float xv = sA_x[batch_offset][k]; float hv = sA_h[batch_offset][k]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < FEATS_PER_THREAD; j++) { sum[j] += xv * sB_x[feat_offset + j][k]; sum[j] += hv * sB_h[feat_offset + j][k]; } } } __syncthreads(); } // Add bias, apply tanh, write output if (b_idx < M) { #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < FEATS_PER_THREAD; j++) { float val = sum[j] + b[f_idx + j]; out[b_idx * N + f_idx + j] = tanhf(val); } } } ``` Wait, there's a problem. In the dot product loop: ```cpp for (int k = 0; k < BK; k++) { float xv = sA_x[batch_offset][k]; float hv = sA_h[batch_offset][k]; for (int j = 0; j < FEATS_PER_THREAD; j++) { sum[j] += xv * sB_x[feat_offset + j][k]; } } ``` sB_x[feat_offset + j][k] - if sB_x is stored as [BN][BK], then for fixed k and varying j, we access sB_x[j0][k], sB_x[j0+1][k], etc. In shared memory, sB_x[j][k] at position j*BK + k. For fixed k and consecutive j, these are consecutive in memory with stride BK. This is good for shared memory access. No bank conflicts if BK is chosen appropriately. For BK=64 and 32 banks, each bank is 4 bytes. Address of sB_x[j][k] is j*64*4 + k*4 = j*256 + k*4. For fixed k, consecutive j differ by 256 bytes. 256 / 4 = 64 banks. Since there are 32 banks, j and j+1 map to the same bank if 256 bytes / 32 = 8 bytes per bank transition... wait, banks are 4 bytes wide, and consecutive 4-byte words go to consecutive banks modulo 32. Address = j*256 + k*4. For k=0: addresses are 0, 256, 512, 768, ... Bank = (address / 4) % 32 = (j*64) % 32. For j=0: bank 0 j=1: bank 0 (since 64 % 32 = 0) OH NO! All j map to the same bank! This causes massive bank conflicts! To fix this, we need to pad BK or use a different access pattern. If we use sB_x[BK][BN] instead, then sB_x[k][j] at address k*BN*4 + j*4. For fixed k and varying j, addresses differ by 4, which goes to consecutive banks. No bank conflicts! So we should transpose the shared memory storage of B. That is: - Load from global memory: W_x[(bn + r) * K + bk + c] where r is feature and c is k. - Store to sB_x[c][r] (swapping indices). - Then in the compute loop, access sB_x[k][j]. This means we load from global memory in one pattern and write to shared memory in a transposed pattern. We can do this with a simple assignment. For the global load to be coalesced: ```cpp for (int i = tid; i < BN * BK; i += n_threads) { int r = i / BK; // feature 0..BN-1 int c = i % BK; // k 0..BK-1 float val = W_x[(bn + r) * K + bk + c]; sB_x[c][r] = val; // transposed store } ``` Wait, if consecutive threads have consecutive tid, and they process: tid=0: r=0, c=0 tid=1: r=0, c=1 tid=2: r=0, c=2 ... tid=63: r=0, c=63 tid=64: r=1, c=0 For W_x access: tid 0: W_x[bn*K + bk + 0] - consecutive c means consecutive memory. Coalesced! tid 64: W_x[(bn+1)*K + bk + 0] - jump of K floats = 64KB. For a warp of 32 threads (tid 0..31): all read W_x[bn*K + bk : bn*K + bk + 31]. Perfectly coalesced. Warp 1 (tid 32..63): reads W_x[bn*K + bk + 32 : bn*K + bk + 63]. Also coalesced. Warp 2 (tid 64..95): reads W_x[(bn+1)*K + bk : (bn+1)*K + bk + 31]. Coalesced but on a different 64KB page. This is good! And for shared memory write: sB_x[c][r]. tid 0: sB_x[0][0] tid 1: sB_x[1][0] tid 2: sB_x[2][0] ... tid 63: sB_x[63][0] In shared memory, sB_x is [BK][BN]. Address = c*BN*4 + r*4 = c*BN*4 (since r=0 for all these). For c=0..63: addresses are 0, BN*4, 2*BN*4, ... If BN=64: stride = 256 bytes. Banks = (addr/4)%32 = (c*64)%32 = 0 for all c (since 64%32=0). BANK CONFLICT! Oh no! Writing to sB_x[c][0] for varying c gives bank conflicts because of the 256-byte stride. To fix the shared memory bank conflicts, we need to either: 1. Pad the second dimension: sB_x[BK][BN + PAD] 2. Or load in a different pattern where consecutive threads write to consecutive banks. If we use sB_x[BK][BN + 1], then address = c*(BN+1)*4 + r*4. For r=0, address = c*(BN+1)*4. For c=0: 0, bank 0 c=1: (65)*4 = 260, bank (260/4)%32 = 65%32 = 1 c=2: 520, bank 520/4=130%32 = 2 ... c=32: 32*65*4 = 8320, bank 8320/4=2080%32 = 0 c=33: bank 1 So with BN=64 and PAD=1 (65 columns), banks cycle every 32. Perfect! No bank conflicts. Actually, the bank of address `addr` is `(addr / 4) % 32` for 4-byte access. For sB_x[c][r] with padding P: address = c*(BN+P)*4 + r*4. Bank = (c*(BN+P) + r) % 32. For consecutive c and r=0: banks are c*(BN+P) % 32. We want BN+P to be odd (or at least co-prime with 32) so that banks cycle. If BN=64, BN+P=65. 65 and 32 are co-prime? gcd(65,32)=1. YES! So banks cycle through all 32 banks with period 32. But for c=0..63 with r=0: banks are 0, 1, 2, ..., 31, 0, 1, ..., 31. Two full cycles. No bank conflicts! Great! So padding by 1 works perfectly. For A shared memory: sA_x[BM][BK+P_A] Addresses: r*(BK+P_A)*4 + c*4. For consecutive c and fixed r: banks are c*(BK+P_A) % 32. If BK=64, we need BK+P_A odd. P_A=1 gives 65, which is co-prime to 32. Good. So shared memory declarations: ```cpp __shared__ float sA_x[BM][BK + 1]; __shared__ float sA_h[BM][BK + 1]; __shared__ float sB_x[BK][BN + 1]; __shared__ float sB_h[BK][BN + 1]; ``` Wait, but sB_x is accessed as sB_x[k][j] in the compute loop. For fixed k and varying j: addresses = k*(BN+1)*4 + j*4. Bank = (k*(BN+1) + j) % 32. For consecutive j, banks are consecutive. PERFECT! No bank conflicts. For fixed j and varying k in the compute loop: Bank = (k*(BN+1) + j) % 32. For consecutive k: banks skip by (BN+1) % 32 = 65 % 32 = 1. So banks are consecutive too! AMAZING! This is the best of both worlds. So with BN+1 = 65 and BK+1 = 65, we get no bank conflicts in either access pattern. Wait, BK+1 = 65. For sA_x[batch][k], fixed batch and varying k: Bank = (batch*(BK+1) + k) % 32 = (batch*65 + k) % 32. For consecutive k, banks differ by 1. Good. For fixed k and varying batch: Bank = (batch*65 + k) % 32. For consecutive batch, banks differ by 65 % 32 = 1. Also good! So padding by 1 gives no bank conflicts for both sA and sB. This is beautiful. Now let's compute shared memory size with these padded dimensions: BM = 16, BK = 64 sA_x: 16 × 65 × 4 = 4160 bytes sA_h: 16 × 65 × 4 = 4160 bytes sB_x: 64 × 65 × 4 = 16640 bytes sB_h: 64 × 65 × 4 = 16640 bytes Total: 41600 bytes = 40.625 KB Double buffer would be ~81KB, which is well under 228KB. Single buffer is ~41KB. With single buffer, we can fit 5 blocks per SM (5 × 41KB = 205KB < 228KB). 5 blocks × 256 threads = 1280 threads = 40 warps per SM. Good for latency hiding! Actually, maximum shared memory per block is 228KB, and per SM is 228KB. So if one block uses 41KB, max concurrent blocks per SM is floor(228/41) = 5. Total blocks for i2h: (256/16) × (16384/64) = 16 × 256 = 4096 blocks. Actually, BN = 64 is the number of output features per block. 16384 / 64 = 256 N-blocks. And 256 / 16 = 16 M-blocks. Total: 4096 blocks. Wait, but how many threads per block? We need enough threads to load all data efficiently. For BM=16, BN=64, BK=64: - Data per K tile: sA_x (16×64) + sA_h (16×64) + sB_x (64×64) + sB_h (64×64) = 1024 + 1024 + 4096 + 4096 = 10240 floats. - At 512 threads, each thread loads 20 floats. Good. - At 256 threads, each thread loads 40 floats. Also good. Let's use 256 threads. Then FEATS_PER_THREAD needs to be such that each thread computes some outputs. Total outputs per block: 16 × 64 = 1024. 1024 / 256 threads = 4 outputs per thread. So FEATS_PER_THREAD = 4. For the compute loop, each thread: - Handles batch_offset = tid % 16 (one batch element) - Handles feat_start = (tid / 16) * 4 (4 consecutive features) And it loops over k=0..63, accumulating: sum[j] += xv * sB_x[k][feat_start + j] + hv * sB_h[k][feat_start + j] This is 4 outputs × 64 iterations × 2 FMA per iteration (x and h) = 512 FMAs per block iteration. Total block iterations = K / BK = 16384 / 64 = 256. Total FMA per thread: 512 × 256 = 131,072 FMAs. Total FMA per block: 131,072 × 256 = 33.5M FMAs. At 33.5M FMAs per block and 4096 blocks: total FMAs = 137.4B (matches our earlier calculation for i2h). If each block takes, say, 500 microseconds at effective 67 GFLOPS (per block? Wait, the whole GPU does 67 TFLOPS, so per block on one SM it's much less). If an SM runs 5 blocks and achieves 500 GFLOPS FP32, time per block = 33.5M FMA / 500GF = 67 microseconds. Total time to process all blocks: 4096 blocks / (132 SMs × 5 blocks/SM) × 67us = (4096/660) × 67us = 6.2 × 67us = 415us. For memory bandwidth: time = 2.15GB / 3.35TB/s = 0.64ms = 640us. So compute time (415us) is less than memory time (640us). Our kernel is memory-bound. At 80% bandwidth efficiency: 640us / 0.8 = 800us. 800us is slower than what TF32 Tensor Cores could achieve (~270us compute bound). But if the reference is ALSO memory-bound or has additional overhead, we might still be competitive. Wait, cuBLAS with TF32 might also be memory-bound. For the i2h single GEMM, total weight + activation reads: - x: 16.8MB - h: 16.8MB (implied in combined) - W: 2.15GB Total: ~2.18GB. Time at 3.35TB/s and 80% efficiency: 2.18GB / 2.68TB/s = 813us. Then h2o: - combined (hidden): 16.8MB - W_o: 536MB Total: ~553MB. Time: 553MB / 2.68TB/s = 207us. Total theoretical minimum memory time: 813 + 207 = 1020us. Plus kernel overhead, concat, tanh, etc. If our custom kernel achieves close to 80% bandwidth and fuses everything into 2 kernels with minimal overhead: - i2h: read x (16.8MB) + h (16.8MB) + W_x (1.07GB) + W_h (1.07GB) = 2.17GB. Time: ~810us at 80%. - h2o: read hidden (16.8MB) + W_o (536MB) = 553MB. Time: ~207us. - Total: ~1017us. This is essentially the same memory time as the reference! But our kernel might have less overhead and better caching. Actually, if we don't write hidden_new to global memory and instead keep it around for h2o in the same kernel or reuse registers... wait, h2o needs the full hidden_new (16.8MB), which doesn't fit in shared memory. So we must write it to global memory between kernels. Unless we have a single kernel where blocks compute hidden_new for some features, then immediately compute output. But to compute output, we need ALL hidden_new features, not just some. So a block can't compute output unless it computes all 16384 hidden features for its batch elements. If a block computes all 16384 hidden features for BM batch elements: - For BM=4, it computes 4 × 16384 = 65536 hidden features. - Then it computes 4 × 8192 = 32768 output features. - The block does 4 × (2 × 16384² + 16384 × 8192) = 4 × (536M + 134M) = 2.68B FMAs. - At 500 GFLOPS per SM, this takes 5.4ms per block. - Total blocks: 256/4 = 64. - 64 blocks on 132 SMs: total time ~5.4ms × 64/132 = 2.6ms. Actually, each block takes 5.4ms, but they run in parallel across SMs. However, only 64 blocks means poor parallelism - many SMs are idle. So computing everything in one block is bad for parallelism. Back to the two-kernel approach. I think the theoretical analysis shows that without Tensor Cores, we can't beat the reference by much (if at all) if the reference achieves good bandwidth. However, the reference has overhead from concat, additional kernel launches, and Python that might give us a few percent. Let me write the kernel, compile it, and test. Actually, I just realized I could use __ldg for global loads. This uses the read-only data cache (texture cache) and can improve performance for read-only data like weights. Also, for better instruction-level parallelism, I can unroll the inner loop. Another idea: process 2 or 4 K tiles per loop iteration to have more independent instructions for the compiler to schedule. Let me write the code now. Actually, I want to add one more optimization. Since we have two weight matrices (W_x and W_h) that are the same size, we can load them together or interleave their computation to maximize instruction throughput. Also, since each output requires sum_k x[b,k]*W_x[j,k] + h[b,k]*W_h[j,k], we can compute both dot products in the same loop: ```cpp for (int k = 0; k < BK; k++) { float xv = sA_x[k]; float hv = sA_h[k]; sum1 += xv * sB_x[k][f1]; sum1 += hv * sB_h[k][f1]; sum2 += xv * sB_x[k][f2]; sum2 += hv * sB_h[k][f2]; ... } ``` With 4 output features per thread, that's 16 FMAs per iteration. At 2 FMAs per cycle, that's 8 cycles. But if the compiler can dual-issue loads and FMAs, and we have enough warps, this might be fine. Actually, 16 FMAs per thread per k-iteration is a lot. For BK=64, that's 1024 FMAs per thread per K-tile. With 256 K-tiles, total 262K FMAs per thread. At 2GHz and 2 FMAs per cycle, one thread can do 4 GFMA/s. So 262K FMAs takes 65.5us. But with 256 threads per block, the block should finish faster due to parallel execution. At 100% theoretical utilization of a single SM (which has many more execution units), the block could finish in just a few microseconds for the compute part. But memory bandwidth dominates. OK, I'll write the code and we'll see. For step 2 (output), it's similar but simpler (only one input and one weight). Let me write the complete Python module. Actually, wait. I just realized that if the reference uses a single large GEMM with cuBLAS, it might be using a specific algorithm that's optimized for K=32768. Our two smaller GEMMs might have different performance. But we're doing them in a single custom kernel, which saves the intermediate write. Let me also consider: what if there's a simpler way to use cuBLAS with accumulation? If we use torch.addmm properly: ```python h_new = torch.addmm(self.b_h, x, self.W_x_t) # h_new = x @ W_x_t + b_h torch.addmm(h_new, self.hidden, self.W_h_t, beta=1, out=h_new) # h_new += hidden @ W_h_t self.hidden = torch.tanh(h_new) output = torch.addmm(self.b_o, self.hidden, self.W_o_t) ``` This avoids creating temporary tensors for the intermediate results (except h_new which is reused). The first addmm creates h_new, the second accumulates in-place (via `out=h_new`). This is very efficient! cuBLAS reads h_new and overwrites it. But it still reads h_new from global memory, does the GEMM, and writes back. In our custom kernel, we accumulate both contributions in registers/shared memory before writing to global memory, saving the intermediate write and read. This difference is: - Reference split approach: write h_new_part1 (16.8MB), read h_new_part1 + write h_new_full (33.6MB traffic). - Our custom kernel: no intermediate write/read. So we save ~33.6MB of activation traffic. At 3.35TB/s, that's 10 microseconds. Small but nice. Actually, I realize one more thing: in our custom kernel, we're reading W_x and W_h in the SAME loop. So we interleave their memory accesses, which might help with memory parallelism. But the total weight bandwidth is the same. Given all this analysis, my plan is: 1. Write a custom C++ CUDA kernel with the tiling approach and bank-conflict-free shared memory. 2. Pre-transpose weights in __init__ for optimal memory layout. 3. Also be sure to handle the hidden state properly (register as buffer, etc.). 4. Test, check correctness, profile. 5. If too slow, try optimizations like async copy or Tensor Cores. Let me draft the code now. Actually, one more trick: for loading A tiles (x and h), since they're small (16 × 64 = 1024 floats each), we can load them very efficiently. And for W_x and W_h tiles (64 × 64 = 4096 floats each), we use float4 to load 4 floats at a time. For float4 loads of W: Each thread can load 4 floats using `*(float4*)(&W[(bn + r) * K + bk + c])`. But our loading loop maps threads to (r, c) positions. If each thread loads a float4, it handles 4 consecutive c values. Then we need BN * BK / 4 = 4096 / 4 = 1024 thread-iterations. With 256 threads, each thread does 4 loads. That's good.
